ðŸ™ A huge thanks for all the kind words guys! As much as I love Sendai, unfortunately it's one of the most seismically active regions in Japan. I'd be lying if I said I wasn't on edge still a day later, given it was such a shocking and intense earthquake. Most earthquakes start as a faint rumble and then get worse and worse. This one felt like a bomb had gone off a few floors below and catapulted the entire floor upwards in a very sudden and unpleasant manner. Since then we've had quite a few aftershocks, such as the one recorded at the end of the video. But I think above all it's important to be grateful that despite the huge earthquake casualties were pretty low and damage fairly minimal, with power and water outages fixed incredibly fast. It's a testament to Japan's astonishing engineering and emergency services.  As for me, it's time to get back to work scrubbing up the studio! â­ For more updates I recommend following Abroad in Japan on Twitter.  (Youtube isn't great for posting minute-by-minute updates). â­ https://twitter.com/AbroadInJapan

For those who come to Japan, let me explain how to respond to earthquakes, which Japanese people have been taught since elementary school.  When an earthquake strikes in Japan, there are almost no cases of buildings collapsing. Conversely, if you go outside, wires and other debris will fall from above, so it's safer not to go outside.  First of all, if you are using fire, put it out quickly, and then protect your head by hiding your head under the table in case the furniture falls over in the house. Fire and head injuries reduce post-earthquake survival. Once the tremors have stopped completely, please check on TV or the Internet whether there is a possibility of a secondary disaster such as a tsunami or a landslide. If an evacuation order has been issued for your current location, immediately evacuate to the designated evacuation site.  Panic is dangerous. Do not make loud noises and act calmly, as it may make not only you but also the people around you uneasy and cause a mass panic.  However, secondary disasters such as tsunamis and landslides require more attention than earthquakes

In future, maybe you could have some of the sticky material that museums put under ceramics to hold them in place for preservation purposes (it's like a reusable glue and available through Amazon). I think the substance is called Puddy or it could be tack. It's been a while, but it's like a stronger version of the sticky stuff that can tack up posters on walls (but it is specifically meant for holding breakable things in place on flat surfaces). It's usually clear/colorless, but it may save you money in future. Love your show and I hope you continue to feel the support your viewers feel for you where you are.

I still remember that night years ago, I had the flu pretty bad and was sitting up in bed feeling miserable and my phone started to buzz. Looked at the screen and recognized my brother's number - calling from Nagoya, Japan. When I answered the phone he sounded a bit shaken, said he was taking a taxi home from work early because they had just had a pretty big shaker and to let mom and the rest of the family know he was okay. He couldn't stay on the phone long, so I thanked him for the heads-up and told him I hoped his fish tank was okay and that there wasn't too much damage. He said he wasn't sure about the tank but so far where he was looked okay, and from how bad it was he was pretty sure it had been fairly local. After he hung up, I told my mom who kinda shrugged it off - I guess he had told her a few hours ago they had a small earthquake and she thought that he was referring to that again (it was pretty late so that seemed to sort make sense.) then I texted my other brother and his wife the update and went to bed.  The next morning it was a text from my sister in law that woke me up, basically saying something along the lines of "OMG I'm so glad you messaged us, I just turned on the morning news and was freaking out!"  So of course I woke up mom and dad and we turned on the tv. My brother had been very wrong about where the earthquake had been centered, it had not come from anywhere near Nagoya, but instead was from much further north and had been so much worse than he had known then. And yes, it was the Tohoku Earthquake.   Later he told me that even in being Japan they didn't know how bad the damage was, or hear about the tsunami until a day or two later - there was enough damage spread wide enough along the coast that, in all of the confusion the news just didn't get out super fast, and what did get out was jumbled and confused. I will never forget the feeling of that morning.

Kintsugi (é‡‘ç¶™ãŽ, "golden joinery"), also known as kintsukuroi (é‡‘ç¹•ã„, "golden repair"),[1] is the Japanese art of repairing broken pottery by mending the areas of breakage with lacquer dusted or mixed with powdered gold, silver, or platinum; the method is similar to the maki-e technique.[2][3][4] As a philosophy, it treats breakage and repair as part of the history of an object, rather than something to disguise.[5]

I remember my 1st big earthquake when I Nepal few years ago. It was terrifying thought we were going to die. Hope everyone in the affected place is alright.
About ten years ago in Chile we had a huge earthquake. It was around 3am. I woke up, realized it was an earthquake and stood up. The ground was moving as if we were on a boat, I couldn't stand. I tried to go to my sisters' room and I got hit by the walls like 5 times. We somehow got on top of a bed and the three of us hugged as shit was flying over. Books, a sheet of glass that was on top of a furniture, the tv, computer, clothes... Everything flying and then ended up on the ground.   All that while listening to our whole kitchen breaking. When it ended a lot of neighbours were trapped inside their apartments, so we kicked their doors down.   The cleaning afterwards is the worst. Our tv fell on top of our dvd and we were left with no tv and a flat dvd player. We had no glasses, lost like half of our dishes and we had a lot of wine and pisco that broke, about 15 bottles of booze or more. All the liquid was soaking the kitchen cabinets, lol. Imagine the smell. Then with every "replica" (the small baby earthquakes that come after the big one) we got petrified. We slept together on one bed for 2 weeks because my dad was on a trip and he couldn't come back to the house. We felt very unsafe.  Eventually things got back to normal, but once you live an earthquake you develop a really cautious mindset.

As head of the division responsible for China, Hong Kong, Macao, Taiwan, and Mongolia, he said the EEAS is seriously concerned about the organ harvesting in China.  Porter noted that the hearing attendees had heard many findings from various investigations, including disturbing allegations ranging from sexual abuse, torture, DNA testing, and illegal detention to organ harvesting in China. More and more evidence collected has shown that Falun Gong practitioners, minority ethnic groups, and faith groups in China are being persecuted in China.  Porter also said that the EU strongly condemned the crime of organ harvesting in China because it is inhumane and immoral. Respect for human rights is not optional, it is a basic requirement that we should all follow, including in the field of organ donation and organ transplantation. He emphasized that human rights conditions will continue to be a focal point at multilateral forums for EU human rights actions.

Rough... well, the set will have even more character now. I was surprised to see so many of it was ceramic still... I really thought it was mostly plastic stuff with the world famous plastic display food! xD The whole thing about making things ready for an earthquake is also fairly counterintuitive... at least building wise, perhaps even for stuff like interior decoration. You'd think having everything tied to walls and made of strong materials is the right way of doing it, but it often ends up resulting in other types of damages because of how violent and sudden the shaking is.  It's an insane amount of energy to dissipate somehow, to the point that if, for instance, you tie electronics too firmly to a wall, it might end up more badly damaged than if it had just fallen from a stand or shelf. Anyways, glad to see all of you guys well... shitty aftershocks incoming, but hopefully the worst has passed. Also... very close to 11yrs after Tohoku. I survived obviously :P but do not wish anyone to go through it. I was at home at the time, so ran outside away from any potential debris that could fall on my head, or a roof falling on me... people aged 90+ ran outside crying... that was one of the most intense moments in my life... and then aftershocks... eventually after a day I just got used to earthquakes, and every time there was an earthquake (especially at night) I was just like ohhhh shut up, and keep going back to sleep :P During the day just stood till it passed. At first it is scary AF, but then the more you experience earthquakes the less scary those are.

Ouch. So sorry, glad you're safe. I was in one in southern Rwanda in 2008. It was only an aftershock, but it brought down the roof of the hotel opposite. Omg
ðŸ™ A huge thanks for all the kind words guys! As much as I love Sendai, unfortunately it's one of the most seismically active regions in Japan. I'd be lying if I said I wasn't on edge still a day later, given it was such a shocking and intense earthquake. Most earthquakes start as a faint rumble and then get worse and worse. This one felt like a bomb had gone off a few floors below and catapulted the entire floor upwards in a very sudden and unpleasant manner. Since then we've had quite a few aftershocks, such as the one recorded at the end of the video. But I think above all it's important to be grateful that despite the huge earthquake casualties were pretty low and damage fairly minimal, with power and water outages fixed incredibly fast. It's a testament to Japan's astonishing engineering and emergency services.  As for me, it's time to get back to work scrubbing up the studio! â­ For more updates I recommend following Abroad in Japan on Twitter.  (Youtube isn't great for posting minute-by-minute updates). â­ https://twitter.com/AbroadInJapan
